<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Second_world_war_asia_1937-1942_map_en6.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Second_world_war_asia_1937-1942_map_en6.png/220px-Second_world_war_asia_1937-1942_map_en6.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="169" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Second_world_war_asia_1937-1942_map_en6.png/330px-Second_world_war_asia_1937-1942_map_en6.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Second_world_war_asia_1937-1942_map_en6.png/440px-Second_world_war_asia_1937-1942_map_en6.png 2x" data-file-width="1298" data-file-height="998"></a><figcaption>A map of the Japanese advance from 1937 to 1942</figcaption></figure>
<p>Over the next two years the Allies slowly captured one island base after another, trying to get closer to Japan itself, where it planned to launch massive strategic air attacks and, only if absolutely necessary, execute a ground invasion. By July 1944, the US <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Guam_(1944)" title="Battle of Guam (1944)">recaptured Guam</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tinian" title="Battle of Tinian">captured Tinian</a>, which finally put them in attacking range of mainland Japan using the new <a href="/wiki/Boeing_B-29_Superfortress" title="Boeing B-29 Superfortress">Boeing B-29</a> bombers.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>109<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Despite the advances of the Allies, massive fighting was still taking place, both in <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">South-East Asia</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Burma_Campaign_1944" class="mw-redirect" title="Burma Campaign 1944">Japanese offensive in India</a> and in the Pacific, with the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Philippine_Sea" title="Battle of the Philippine Sea">Battle of the Philippine Sea</a>, where Japan suffered major losses and definitively lost the ability to rely on <a href="/wiki/Aircraft_carrier" title="Aircraft carrier">aircraft carriers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>110<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> In later October, a combined attack of American and Australian forces took on the Imperial Japanese Navy during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Leyte_Gulf" title="Battle of Leyte Gulf">Battle of Leyte Gulf</a> in what was to be the largest naval battle of World War II and possibly the <a href="/wiki/Largest_naval_battle_in_history" title="Largest naval battle in history">largest naval battle of all time</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>111<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The Allied victory achieved its stated goal of further severing oil supplied to the Japanese army, which was already struggling to fuel the remainder of its naval power.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>112<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The battle of Leyte Gulf is also notable as the first battle in which Japanese aircraft carried out organized <i><a href="/wiki/Kamikaze" title="Kamikaze">kamikaze</a></i> attacks.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>113<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>By early 1945, the Americans set their eyes on the Japanese island of Iwo Jima, which was of great strategic importance, being situated halfway between <a href="/wiki/Tokyo" title="Tokyo">Tokyo</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mariana_Islands" title="Mariana Islands">Mariana Islands</a>. Capturing the island meant not only a better launch station for aerial attacks against mainland Japan, but also the prevention of its use as an early-warning station against air raids. The Japanese were expecting an attack and have been heavily fortifying the entire island for over a year.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>114<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Despite the American troops outnumbering the Japanese to a rate of more than 3 to 1, the USA suffered heavy casualties in the 36 days of fighting, with over 6,800 Marines killed and another 20,000 wounded,<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>115<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> leading some historians to question the strategic worth of the action.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>116<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> With Japan struggling to defend the homeland islands, counteroffensives in other parts of the Japanese Empire became more feasible, with important battles taking place in <a href="/wiki/Burma_Campaign_1944%E2%80%9345" class="mw-redirect" title="Burma Campaign 194445">Burma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Borneo_campaign_(1945)" class="mw-redirect" title="Borneo campaign (1945)">Borneo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Second_Guangxi_Campaign" class="mw-redirect" title="Second Guangxi Campaign">China</a>.
</p><p>Continuing to come nearer the main Japanese islands, on 1 April 1945 the US Marines mounted the largest <a href="/wiki/Amphibious_assault" class="mw-redirect" title="Amphibious assault">amphibious assault</a> of the Pacific Theater on the island of <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Okinawa" title="Battle of Okinawa">Okinawa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>117<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> After a long campaign of <a href="/wiki/Leapfrogging_(military)" class="mw-redirect" title="Leapfrogging (military)">island hopping</a>, the Allies were planning to use <a href="/wiki/Kadena_Air_Base" title="Kadena Air Base">Kadena Air Base</a> on the large island of Okinawa as a base for <a href="/wiki/Operation_Downfall" title="Operation Downfall">Operation Downfall</a>, the planned invasion of the <a href="/wiki/Japanese_home_islands" class="mw-redirect" title="Japanese home islands">Japanese home islands</a> which were only 340 miles away.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>118<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The battle was one of the bloodiest in the Pacific, with approximately 160,000 casualties on both sides: at least 75,000 Allied and 84,166117,000 Japanese. Around half of the island's population of 300,000 were killed, committed suicide or went missing.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>119<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> 
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:B-29_Enola_Gay_w_Crews.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/B-29_Enola_Gay_w_Crews.jpg/220px-B-29_Enola_Gay_w_Crews.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/B-29_Enola_Gay_w_Crews.jpg/330px-B-29_Enola_Gay_w_Crews.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/B-29_Enola_Gay_w_Crews.jpg/440px-B-29_Enola_Gay_w_Crews.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="960"></a><figcaption>Enola Gay's ground crew with mission commander <a href="/wiki/Paul_Tibbets" title="Paul Tibbets">Paul Tibbets</a> in the center.</figcaption></figure>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Final_days_2">Final days</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Key_events_of_the_20th_century&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Final days"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The assaults on both Iwo Jima and Okinawa proved incredibly costly in terms of American lives and president Truman was horrified at the prospect of Operation Downfall, a planned boots-on-the-ground invasion of mainland Japan that was estimated to lead to over a million casualties among American soldiers.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>120<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Despite a devastating campaign of fire-bombing in Tokyo and dozens of other cities, the Japanese showed no sign of planning to surrender. Following the <a href="/wiki/Potsdam_Declaration" title="Potsdam Declaration">Potsdam Declaration</a> of 26 July, where the Allies threatened "prompt and utter destruction" should Japan not surrender,<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>121<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> the decision was made to resort to the first use of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">atomic bombs</a>. On 6 August, the 393d Bombardment Squadron B-29 <i><a href="/wiki/Enola_Gay" title="Enola Gay">Enola Gay</a></i>, took off from Tinian and dropped the bomb on the city of Hiroshima. Three days later, a second bomb was dropped on Nagasaki. The two bombings killed 129,000226,000 people, most of them civilians.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>122<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> On 9 August, the Soviet Union also invaded Manchukuo in what was to be the last campaign of the war.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>123<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>On 10 August the "sacred decision" was made by Japanese Cabinet to accept the <a href="/wiki/Potsdam_Declaration" title="Potsdam Declaration">Potsdam terms</a> and on 15 August <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Hirohito" class="mw-redirect" title="Emperor Hirohito">Emperor Hirohito</a> broadcast to the nation and to the world at large the rescript of surrender, ending the Second World War.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>124<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The formal <a href="/wiki/Japanese_Instrument_of_Surrender" title="Japanese Instrument of Surrender">Japanese Instrument of Surrender</a> was signed on 2 September 1945, on the battleship <a href="/wiki/USS_Missouri_(BB-63)" title="USS Missouri (BB-63)">USS <i>Missouri</i></a>, in <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_Bay" title="Tokyo Bay">Tokyo Bay</a>. The surrender was accepted by General Douglas MacArthur as <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Commander_of_the_Allied_Powers" class="mw-redirect" title="Supreme Commander of the Allied Powers">Supreme Commander of the Allied Powers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>125<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="The_Holocaust">The Holocaust</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Key_events_of_the_20th_century&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: The Holocaust"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/The_Holocaust" title="The Holocaust">The Holocaust</a></div>
<p>The Holocaust (which roughly means "great fire") was the deliberate, systematic murder of millions of Jews and other minorities during World War II by <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Hitler's Nazi regime</a> in Europe. Several differing views exist regarding whether it was intended to occur from the war's beginning, or if the plans for it came about later.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>126<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>127<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Regardless, persecution of Jews extended well before the war even started, such as in the <i><a href="/wiki/Kristallnacht" title="Kristallnacht">Kristallnacht</a></i> (literally "Crystal Night", Night of Broken Glass).<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>128<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/The_Nazis" class="mw-redirect" title="The Nazis">The Nazis</a> used propaganda to great effect to stir up antisemitic feelings within ordinary Germans. Many people, including politicians and historians, consider the Holocaust to be the worst event in history, and have described Hitler, <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Party" title="Nazi Party">his followers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">his regime</a> as <a href="/wiki/Evil" title="Evil">evil</a>.
</p>
<figure class="mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Buchenwald_Slave_Laborers_Liberation.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Buchenwald_Slave_Laborers_Liberation.jpg/200px-Buchenwald_Slave_Laborers_Liberation.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="162" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Buchenwald_Slave_Laborers_Liberation.jpg/300px-Buchenwald_Slave_Laborers_Liberation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dc/Buchenwald_Slave_Laborers_Liberation.jpg/400px-Buchenwald_Slave_Laborers_Liberation.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2699" data-file-height="2190"></a><figcaption>Slave laborers at the <a href="/wiki/Buchenwald_concentration_camp" title="Buchenwald concentration camp">Buchenwald concentration camp</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<p>After the conquest of Poland, the <a href="/wiki/Third_Reich" class="mw-redirect" title="Third Reich">Third Reich</a>, which had previously deported Jews and other "undesirables", suddenly had within its borders the largest concentration of Jews in the world. The solution was to round up Jews and place them in <a href="/wiki/Nazi_concentration_camps" title="Nazi concentration camps">Nazi concentration camps</a> or in <a href="/wiki/Ghetto" title="Ghetto">ghettos</a>, cordoned off sections of cities where Jews were forced to live in deplorable conditions, often with tens of thousands starving to death, and the bodies decaying in the streets.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>129<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>130<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> As appalling as this sounds, they were the lucky ones. After the invasion of the Soviet Union, armed killing squads of <a href="/wiki/SS" class="mw-redirect" title="SS">SS</a> men known as <i><a href="/wiki/Einsatzgruppen" title="Einsatzgruppen">Einsatzgruppen</a></i> systematically rounded up Jews and murdered an estimated one million Jews within the country.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>131<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> As barbaric and inhuman as this seems, it was too slow and inefficient by Nazi standards.
</p><p>In 1942, the top leadership met in <a href="/wiki/Wannsee" title="Wannsee">Wannsee</a>, a suburb of Berlin, and began to plan a more efficient way to slaughter the Jews.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>132<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The Nazis created a system of <a href="/wiki/Extermination_camp" title="Extermination camp">extermination camps</a> throughout Poland, and began rounding up Jews from the Soviet Union, and from the Ghettos.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>133<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>134<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Not only were Jews shot or gassed to death en masse, but they were forced to provide slave labor and they were used in horrific medical experiments (see <a href="/wiki/Human_experimentation_in_Nazi_Germany" class="mw-redirect" title="Human experimentation in Nazi Germany">Human experimentation in Nazi Germany</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>135<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Out of the widespread condemnation of the Nazis' medical experiments, the <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg_Code" title="Nuremberg Code">Nuremberg Code</a> of medical ethics was devised.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>136<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The Nazis took a sadistic pleasure in the death camps; the entrance to the most notorious camp, <a href="/wiki/Auschwitz" class="mw-redirect" title="Auschwitz">Auschwitz</a>, stated <i>"Arbeit Macht Frei"</i>"Work Sets You Free".<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>137<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> In the end, six million Jews and up to 5 million homosexuals, <a href="/wiki/Jehovah%27s_Witness" class="mw-redirect" title="Jehovah's Witness">Jehovah's Witnesses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romani_people" title="Romani people">Roma</a> and political prisoners were killed by various means, mainly in the death camps.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>138<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>139<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>140<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> An additional several million Soviet and other Allied prisoners of war died in camps and holding areas.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>141<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>142<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>There is some controversy over whether ordinary Germans knew about the Holocaust.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>143<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> It appears that many Germans knew about the concentration camps; such things were prominently displayed in magazines and newspapers.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>144<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> In many places, Jews had to walk past towns and villages on their way to work as slaves in German industry. In any case, Allied soldiers reported that the smell of the camps carried for miles.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>145<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> A very small number of people <a href="/wiki/Holocaust_denial" title="Holocaust denial">deny the Holocaust occurred</a> entirely, though these claims have been routinely discredited by mainstream historians.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>146<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="The_Nuclear_Age_begins">The Nuclear Age begins</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Key_events_of_the_20th_century&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: The Nuclear Age begins"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_nuclear_weapons" title="History of nuclear weapons">History of nuclear weapons</a></div>
<p>During the 1930s, innovations in <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a> made it apparent that it could be possible to develop <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapons" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear weapons">nuclear weapons</a> of incredible power using <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_reactions" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear reactions">nuclear reactions</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>147<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> When World War II broke out, scientists and advisors among the Allies feared that Nazi Germany may have been trying to develop its own atomic weapons, and the United States and the United Kingdom pooled their efforts in what became known as the <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Project" title="Manhattan Project">Manhattan Project</a> to beat them to it. At the secret <a href="/wiki/Los_Alamos_National_Laboratory" title="Los Alamos National Laboratory">Los Alamos laboratory</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a>, scientist <a href="/wiki/Robert_Oppenheimer" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert Oppenheimer">Robert Oppenheimer</a> led a team of the world's top scientists to develop the first nuclear weapons, the first of which was tested at the <a href="/wiki/Trinity_site" class="mw-redirect" title="Trinity site">Trinity site</a> in July 1945.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>148<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> However, Germany had surrendered in May 1945, and it had been discovered that the <a href="/wiki/German_nuclear_energy_project" class="mw-redirect" title="German nuclear energy project">German atomic bomb program</a> had not been very close to success.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>149<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Trinity_Test_Fireball_16ms.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Trinity_Test_Fireball_16ms.jpg/220px-Trinity_Test_Fireball_16ms.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="148" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Trinity_Test_Fireball_16ms.jpg/330px-Trinity_Test_Fireball_16ms.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Trinity_Test_Fireball_16ms.jpg/440px-Trinity_Test_Fireball_16ms.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1483" data-file-height="1000"></a><figcaption>The first <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_explosion" title="Nuclear explosion">nuclear explosion</a>, named "<a href="/wiki/Trinity_site" class="mw-redirect" title="Trinity site">Trinity</a>", was detonated on 16 July 1945.</figcaption></figure><p>The Allied team produced two nuclear weapons for use in the war, one powered by <a href="/wiki/Uranium-235" title="Uranium-235">uranium-235</a> and the other by <a href="/wiki/Plutonium" title="Plutonium">plutonium</a> as <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fission" title="Nuclear fission">fissionable</a> material, named "<a href="/wiki/Little_Boy" title="Little Boy">Little Boy</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Fat_Man" title="Fat Man">Fat Man</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>150<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> These were dropped on the Japanese cities of <a href="/wiki/Hiroshima" title="Hiroshima">Hiroshima</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nagasaki" title="Nagasaki">Nagasaki</a> on 6 and 9 August 1945 each. This, in combination with the Soviet invasion of Japanese-controlled territory, convinced the Japanese government to surrender unconditionally. These two weapons remain the only two nuclear weapons ever used against other countries in war.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>151<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Nuclear weapons brought an entirely new and <a href="/wiki/Damocles" title="Damocles">terrifying possibility</a> to <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_warfare" title="Nuclear warfare">warfare</a> of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_holocaust" title="Nuclear holocaust">nuclear annihilation</a>, because it was feared that they would bring about the <a href="/wiki/Human_extinction" title="Human extinction">human extinction</a> overnight. While at first the United States held a monopoly on the production of nuclear weapons, the Soviet Union, with some assistance from espionage, managed to detonate its first weapon (dubbed "<a href="/wiki/Joe-1" class="mw-redirect" title="Joe-1">Joe-1</a>" by the West) in August 1949.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>152<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>153<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The post-war relations between the two, which had already been deteriorating, began to rapidly disintegrate. Soon the two were locked in a massive stockpiling of nuclear weapons. The United States began a crash program to develop the first <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_bomb" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydrogen bomb">hydrogen bomb</a> in 1950, and detonated its first <a href="/wiki/Thermonuclear" class="mw-redirect" title="Thermonuclear">thermonuclear</a> weapon in 1952. This new weapon was alone over 400 times as powerful as the weapons used against Japan. The Soviet Union detonated a primitive thermonuclear weapon in 1953 and a full-fledged one in 1955.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>154<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Trident_II_missile_image.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Trident_II_missile_image.jpg/200px-Trident_II_missile_image.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="241" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Trident_II_missile_image.jpg/300px-Trident_II_missile_image.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Trident_II_missile_image.jpg/400px-Trident_II_missile_image.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2486" data-file-height="3000"></a><figcaption>Nuclear missiles and computerized launch systems increased the range and scope of possible <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_warfare" title="Nuclear warfare">nuclear war</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The conflict continued to escalate, with the major superpowers developing long-range missiles (such as the <a href="/wiki/ICBM" class="mw-redirect" title="ICBM">ICBM</a>) and a <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_strategy" title="Nuclear strategy">nuclear strategy</a> which guaranteed that any use of the nuclear weapons would be suicide for the attacking nation (<a href="/wiki/Mutually_Assured_Destruction" class="mw-redirect" title="Mutually Assured Destruction">Mutually Assured Destruction</a>). The creation of <a href="/wiki/Ballistic_Missile_Early_Warning_System" title="Ballistic Missile Early Warning System">early warning systems</a> put the control of these weapons into the hands of newly created computers, and they served as a tense backdrop throughout the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>155<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Since the 1940s there were concerns about the rising <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_proliferation" title="Nuclear proliferation">proliferation of nuclear weapons</a> to new countries, which was seen as being destabilizing to international relations, spurring regional <a href="/wiki/Arms_race" title="Arms race">arms races</a>, and generally increasing the likelihood of some form of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_warfare" title="Nuclear warfare">nuclear war</a>. Eventually, nine nations would overtly develop nuclear weapons, and still maintain stockpiles today: the <a href="/wiki/The_United_States_and_weapons_of_mass_destruction" class="mw-redirect" title="The United States and weapons of mass destruction">United States</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Russia_and_weapons_of_mass_destruction" title="Russia and weapons of mass destruction">Soviet Union</a> (and later Russia would inherit these), the <a href="/wiki/The_United_Kingdom_and_weapons_of_mass_destruction" class="mw-redirect" title="The United Kingdom and weapons of mass destruction">United Kingdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/France_and_weapons_of_mass_destruction" title="France and weapons of mass destruction">France</a>, <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China_and_weapons_of_mass_destruction" class="mw-redirect" title="People's Republic of China and weapons of mass destruction">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/India_and_weapons_of_mass_destruction" title="India and weapons of mass destruction">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan_and_weapons_of_mass_destruction" title="Pakistan and weapons of mass destruction">Pakistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>156<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/South_Africa_and_weapons_of_mass_destruction" title="South Africa and weapons of mass destruction">South Africa</a> developed six crude weapons in the 1980s (which it later dismantled),<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>157<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Israel_and_weapons_of_mass_destruction" title="Israel and weapons of mass destruction">Israel</a> almost certainly developed nuclear weapons though it never confirmed nor denied it.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>158<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The creation of the <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_Non-proliferation_Treaty" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear Non-proliferation Treaty">Nuclear Non-proliferation Treaty</a> in 1968 was an attempt to curtail such proliferation,<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>159<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> but a number of countries developed nuclear weapons since it was signed (and many did not sign it), and several other countries, including <a href="/wiki/Libya_and_weapons_of_mass_destruction" title="Libya and weapons of mass destruction">Libya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iran_and_weapons_of_mass_destruction" title="Iran and weapons of mass destruction">Iran</a> were suspected of having clandestine nuclear weapons programs.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>160<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>161<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="The_post-war_world">The post-war world</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Key_events_of_the_20th_century&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: The post-war world"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Post%E2%80%93World_War_II_economic_expansion" title="PostWorld War II economic expansion">PostWorld War II economic expansion</a></div>
<p>Following World War II, the majority of the industrialized world lay in ruins as a result of aerial bombings, naval bombardment, and protracted land campaigns. The United States was a notable exception to this; barring <a href="/wiki/Pearl_Harbor" title="Pearl Harbor">Pearl Harbor</a> and some minor incidents, the U.S. had suffered no attacks upon its territory.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>162<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The United States and the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, which, despite the devastation of its most populated areas, rebuilt quickly, found themselves the world's two dominant superpowers.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>163<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Much of Western Europe was rebuilt after the war with assistance from the <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Plan" title="Marshall Plan">Marshall Plan</a>. Germany, chief instigator of the war, was placed under joint military <a href="/wiki/Military_occupation" title="Military occupation">occupation</a> by the United States, Great Britain, France, and the Soviet Union. Berlin, although in Soviet-controlled territory, was also divided among the four powers.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>164<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Occupation of Berlin would continue until 1990. Japan was also placed under U.S. occupation, that would last seven years, until 1952.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>165<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Oddly, these two <a href="/wiki/Axis_Powers" class="mw-redirect" title="Axis Powers">Axis</a> powers, despite military occupation, soon rose to become the second (Japan) and third (West Germany) most powerful economies in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>166<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Following the end of the war, the <a href="/wiki/Allies_of_World_War_II" title="Allies of World War II">Allies</a> famously prosecuted numerous German officials for <a href="/wiki/War_crime" title="War crime">war crimes</a> and other offenses in the <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg_Trials" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuremberg Trials">Nuremberg Trials</a>. Although <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a> had committed suicide, many of his cronies, including <a href="/wiki/Hermann_G%C3%B6ring" title="Hermann Gring">Hermann Gring</a>, were convicted. Less well-known trials of other <a href="/wiki/Axis_Powers" class="mw-redirect" title="Axis Powers">Axis</a> officials also occurred, including the <a href="/wiki/Tokyo_War_Crime_Trial" class="mw-redirect" title="Tokyo War Crime Trial">Tokyo War Crime Trial</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>167<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The failure of the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a> to prevent World War II essentially discredited the organization, and it was dissolved.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>168<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> A new attempt at <a href="/wiki/World_peace" title="World peace">world peace</a> was begun with the founding of the United Nations on 24 October 1945, in San Francisco.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>169<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Today, nearly all countries are members, but despite its many successes, the organization's success at achieving its goal of world peace is disputed. The organization was never given enough power to overcome the conflicting interests and priorities of its member nations.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="The_end_of_empires:_decolonization">The end of empires: decolonization</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Key_events_of_the_20th_century&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: The end of empires: decolonization"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Somare,_Michael_(2008).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Somare%2C_Michael_%282008%29.jpg/220px-Somare%2C_Michael_%282008%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="310" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Somare%2C_Michael_%282008%29.jpg/330px-Somare%2C_Michael_%282008%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Somare%2C_Michael_%282008%29.jpg/440px-Somare%2C_Michael_%282008%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="463" data-file-height="652"></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Michael_Somare" title="Michael Somare">Michael Somare</a>, the first leader of an independent <a href="/wiki/Papua_New_Guinea" title="Papua New Guinea">Papua New Guinea</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Decolonization" title="Decolonization">Decolonization</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Imperialism" title="New Imperialism">New Imperialism</a></div>
<p>Almost all the major nations involved in World War II began shedding their overseas colonies soon after the conflict. The tactics employed by the revolutionaries ranged from <a href="/wiki/Nonviolence" title="Nonviolence">non-violent</a> forms of protest to armed rebellions, depending on the nation involved.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>170<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>171<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Immediately after the war, European powers began a decades-long process of withdrawing from their possessions in Africa and Asia.
</p><p>In India, <a href="/wiki/Mahatma_Gandhi" title="Mahatma Gandhi">Mahatma Gandhi</a> became a global icon for his non-violent struggle to achieve <a href="/wiki/Indian_independence_movement" title="Indian independence movement">Indian independence</a> whereas <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Ali_Jinnah" title="Muhammad Ali Jinnah">Mohamed Ali Jinnah</a> advocated for the independence of a separate state for Muslims. This was achieved in 1947 with the end of British rule in India and the <a href="/wiki/Partition_of_India" title="Partition of India">Partition</a> of the territory into modern-day India and <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>, which would later also further divide, leading to the creation of the <a href="/wiki/People%E2%80%99s_Republic_of_Bangladesh" class="mw-redirect" title="Peoples Republic of Bangladesh">People's Republic of Bangladesh</a> in 1971.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>172<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>173<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Elsewhere in Asia, The United States granted independence to the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>, its major Pacific possession in 1946.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>174<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/French_Indochina" title="French Indochina">French Indochina</a>, armed insurrections forced the French out in the early 1950s, leading to the formation of <a href="/wiki/Laos" title="Laos">Laos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>175<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In Africa, nationalists such as <a href="/wiki/Jomo_Kenyatta" title="Jomo Kenyatta">Jomo Kenyatta</a> of <a href="/wiki/Kenya" title="Kenya">Kenya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kwame_Nkrumah" title="Kwame Nkrumah">Kwame Nkrumah</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ghana" title="Ghana">Ghana</a> led their respective nations to independence from foreign rule.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>176<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Mere decades before, the British Empire controlled almost half of the continent, but by 1968, the only British possession in Africa was Seychelles (which would also become independent in 1976). Between 1956 and 1962, almost 20 African countries achieved their independence from France.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>177<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Through the efforts of <a href="/wiki/Am%C3%ADlcar_Cabral" title="Amlcar Cabral">Amlcar Cabral</a> and others, the Portuguese colonies of <a href="/wiki/Guinea-Bissau" title="Guinea-Bissau">Guinea-Bissau</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cape_Verde" title="Cape Verde">Cape Verde</a>, <a href="/wiki/Angola" title="Angola">Angola</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a> and <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Tom%C3%A9_and_Pr%C3%ADncipe" title="So Tom and Prncipe">So Tom and Prncipe</a> followed suit and achieved independence during the mid-1970s, in what was to be the last important wave of African decolonization.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>178<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 1946, there were 35 member states in the United Nations, but as the newly independent nations joined the organization, by 1970 membership increased to 127.<sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>179<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The emergence of newly independent countries in Africa and Asia that used the borders of the imperial partitions later led to further conflict.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>180<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> In many cases this meant that historically antagonistic ethnic or religious groups now needed to share the same country or that several nations held territorial claims over regions which were more or less arbitrarily divided by European powers.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>181<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>182<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Some conflicts which aroused from these tensions, especially the <a href="/wiki/Nigerian_Civil_War" title="Nigerian Civil War">Nigerian Civil War</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Second_Congo_War" title="Second Congo War">Second Congo War</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Second_Sudanese_Civil_War" title="Second Sudanese Civil War">Second Sudanese Civil War</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh_Liberation_War" title="Bangladesh Liberation War">Bangladesh Liberation War</a> have been among the bloodiest wars of the 20th century.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>183<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="The_Cold_War_(19471991)"><span id="The_Cold_War_.281947.E2.80.931991.29"></span>The Cold War (19471991)</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Key_events_of_the_20th_century&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: The Cold War (19471991)"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:EasternBloc_BorderChange38-48.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/EasternBloc_BorderChange38-48.svg/220px-EasternBloc_BorderChange38-48.svg.png" decoding="async" width="220" height="374" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/EasternBloc_BorderChange38-48.svg/330px-EasternBloc_BorderChange38-48.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/EasternBloc_BorderChange38-48.svg/440px-EasternBloc_BorderChange38-48.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="680"></a><figcaption>Post-war territorial changes in Europe and the formation of the Eastern Bloc</figcaption></figure>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mutual_assured_destruction" title="Mutual assured destruction">Mutual assured destruction</a></div><p>During the Yalta Conference, where the Western, <a href="/wiki/Capitalism" title="Capitalism">capitalist</a> powers, and the <a href="/wiki/Communism" title="Communism">communist</a> Soviet Union agreed on separate spheres of influence in Europe, they set up the stage for a geopolitical rivalry that would dominate international relations for the next five decades.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>184<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> In March 1946, Winston Churchill gave a now famous speech while visiting <a href="/wiki/Westminster_College_(Missouri)" title="Westminster College (Missouri)">Westminster College</a> in the US which is usually credited as the first use of the term <a href="/wiki/Iron_Curtain" title="Iron Curtain">iron curtain</a> to refer to the separation of Soviet and Western areas of influence in Europe:<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>185<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup></p><blockquote><p>From <a href="/wiki/Szczecin" title="Szczecin">Stettin</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Baltic_Sea" title="Baltic Sea">Baltic</a> to <a href="/wiki/Trieste" title="Trieste">Trieste</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Adriatic_Sea" title="Adriatic Sea">Adriatic</a> an iron curtain has descended across the Continent. Behind that line lie all the capitals of the ancient states of Central and Eastern Europe. Warsaw, Berlin, Prague, Vienna, Budapest, Belgrade, Bucharest and Sofia; all these famous cities and the populations around them lie in what I must call the Soviet sphere, and all are subject, in one form or another, not only to Soviet influence but to a very high and in some cases increasing measure of control from Moscow.</p></blockquote><p>Indeed, the Soviet Union had already annexed several countries as <a href="/wiki/Republics_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Republics of the Soviet Union">Soviet Socialist Republics</a> during the war. Eastern <a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a> (<a href="/wiki/Territories_of_Poland_annexed_by_the_Soviet_Union" title="Territories of Poland annexed by the Soviet Union">incorporated</a> into the <a href="/wiki/Byelorussian_Soviet_Socialist_Republic" title="Byelorussian Soviet Socialist Republic">Byelorussian SSR</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ukrainian_Soviet_Socialist_Republic" title="Ukrainian Soviet Socialist Republic">Ukrainian SSR</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>186<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Latvia" title="Latvia">Latvia</a> (which became the <a href="/wiki/Latvian_Soviet_Socialist_Republic" title="Latvian Soviet Socialist Republic">Latvian SSR</a>), <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a> (which became the <a href="/wiki/Estonian_Soviet_Socialist_Republic" title="Estonian Soviet Socialist Republic">Estonian SSR</a>), <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a> ( which became the <a href="/wiki/Lithuanian_Soviet_Socialist_Republic" title="Lithuanian Soviet Socialist Republic">Lithuanian SSR</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>187<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> part of eastern <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> (which became the <a href="/wiki/Karelo-Finnish_Soviet_Socialist_Republic" title="Karelo-Finnish Soviet Socialist Republic">Karelo-Finnish SSR</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>188<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> and eastern <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a> (part of which became the <a href="/wiki/Moldavian_Soviet_Socialist_Republic" title="Moldavian Soviet Socialist Republic">Moldavian SSR</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>189<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> were now wholly part of Soviet Union. Furthermore, between 1945 and 1949, <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Federal_Republic_of_Yugoslavia" title="Socialist Federal Republic of Yugoslavia">Yugoslavia</a>, <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Socialist_Republic_of_Albania" title="People's Socialist Republic of Albania">Albania</a>, <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_Bulgaria" title="People's Republic of Bulgaria">Bulgaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polish_People%27s_Republic" title="Polish People's Republic">Poland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Republic_of_Romania" title="Socialist Republic of Romania">Romania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Czechoslovak_Socialist_Republic" title="Czechoslovak Socialist Republic">Czechoslovakia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_People%27s_Republic" title="Hungarian People's Republic">Hungary</a> and <a href="/wiki/East_Germany" title="East Germany">East Germany</a> saw communist regimes coming to power and their transformation into <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic" class="mw-redirect" title="People's Republic">People's Republics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>190<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> While they remained independent countries and their relation with the Soviet authorities would fluctuate over the next half century, they were widely seen as Soviet <a href="/wiki/Satellite_state" title="Satellite state">satellite states</a>. Outside of Europe, countries that would see the rise of communism and would ally themselves to the communist bloc include <a href="/wiki/Mongolian_People%27s_Republic" title="Mongolian People's Republic">Mongolia</a>, <a href="/wiki/People%27s_Republic_of_China" class="mw-redirect" title="People's Republic of China">China</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>191<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The spread of the communist ideology in general and the Soviet influence in particular made Western leaders nervous and it even lead to Churchill considering a preemptive attack on the USSR even before World War II was formally ended.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>192<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The two rival blocks also coalesced into formal mutual defense organizations, with the formation of the <a href="/wiki/North_Atlantic_Treaty_Organization_(NATO)" class="mw-redirect" title="North Atlantic Treaty Organization (NATO)">North Atlantic Treaty Organization (NATO)</a> in 1949<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>193<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Warsaw_Pact" title="Warsaw Pact">Warsaw Pact</a> in 1955,<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>194<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> each determine to expand its own influence among <a href="/wiki/Third_World" title="Third World">unaligned countries</a> and limit the influence of their rivals.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="War_by_proxy">War by proxy</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Key_events_of_the_20th_century&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: War by proxy"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cuban_Missile_Crisis" title="Cuban Missile Crisis">Cuban Missile Crisis</a></div>
<p>Two wars and a near-war in the 1950s became the focus for capitalist versus communist struggle. The first war was the <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a>, fought between People's Republic of China-backed <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a> and mainly United States-backed <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a>. The Korean Peninsula was a Japanese colony between 1910 and 1945, when Soviet and American troops invaded and divided it along the <a href="/wiki/38th_parallel_north" title="38th parallel north">38th parallel</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>195<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> A communist government controlled the territory north of the border and a capitalist one controlled the South, with both authorities considering the other one illegitimate and claiming sovereignty over the entire peninsula.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>196<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> North Korea's invasion of South Korea on 25 June 1950 led to United Nations intervention.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>197<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> General <a href="/wiki/Douglas_MacArthur" title="Douglas MacArthur">Douglas MacArthur</a> led troops from the United States, Canada, Australia, Great Britain, and other countries in repulsing the Northern invasion. However, the war reached a stalemate after Chinese intervention pushed U.N. forces back, and an <a href="https://en.wikisource.org/wiki/Korean_Armistice_Agreement" class="extiw" title="s:Korean Armistice Agreement">Armistice</a> ended hostilities in July 1953, leaving the two Koreas divided and tense for the rest of the century.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>198<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The second war, the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a>, was perhaps the third most visible war of the 20th century, after World War I and World War II. After the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Dien_Bien_Phu" title="Battle of Dien Bien Phu">French withdrawal</a> from its former colony, on 21 July 1954, Vietnam became partitioned into two halves, much like Korea, along the <a href="/wiki/17th_parallel_north" title="17th parallel north">17th parallel</a>. Fighting between North and South eventually escalated into a regional war. The United States provided aid to South Vietnam<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>199<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> and contributed to propaganda efforts against the North,<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>200<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> but was not directly involved until the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Tonkin_Resolution" title="Gulf of Tonkin Resolution">Gulf of Tonkin Resolution</a>, passed in reaction to a supposed North Vietnamese attack upon American <a href="/wiki/Destroyer" title="Destroyer">destroyers</a>, brought the U.S. into the war as a belligerent.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>201<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The war was initially viewed as a fight to contain communism (see <a href="/wiki/Containment" title="Containment">containment</a>, <a href="/wiki/Truman_Doctrine" title="Truman Doctrine">Truman Doctrine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Domino_Theory" class="mw-redirect" title="Domino Theory">Domino Theory</a>), but, as more Americans were <a href="/wiki/Conscription" title="Conscription">drafted</a> and news of events such as the <a href="/wiki/Tet_Offensive" title="Tet Offensive">Tet Offensive</a> and <a href="/wiki/My_Lai_massacre" title="My Lai massacre">My Lai massacre</a> leaked out, American sentiment turned against the war.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>202<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>203<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">U.S. President</a> <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">Richard Nixon</a> was elected partially on a promise to end the war. This <a href="/wiki/Nixon_Doctrine" title="Nixon Doctrine">Nixon Doctrine</a> involved a gradual pullout of American forces; South Vietnamese units were supposed to replace them, backed up by American air power. The plan went awry, with Nixon  deliberately sabotaging peace talks for political gain,<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>204<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> and the war spilled into neighboring <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a> while South Vietnamese forces were pushed further back.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>205<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Eventually, the U.S. and North Vietnam signed the <a href="/wiki/Paris_Peace_Accords" title="Paris Peace Accords">Paris Peace Accords</a>, ending U.S. involvement in the war.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>206<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> With the threat of U.S. retaliation gone, the North proceeded to violate the ceasefire and invaded the South with full military force. <a href="/wiki/Saigon" class="mw-redirect" title="Saigon">Saigon</a> was captured on 30 April 1975, and Vietnam was unified under Communist rule a year later, effectively bringing an end to one of the most unpopular wars of all time.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>207<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>208<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Cuban_Missile_Crisis" title="Cuban Missile Crisis">Cuban Missile Crisis</a> illustrates just how close to the brink of <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_warfare" title="Nuclear warfare">nuclear war</a> the world came during the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>. <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a>, under <a href="/wiki/Fidel_Castro" title="Fidel Castro">Fidel Castro</a>'s socialist government, had formed close ties with the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>209<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> This was obviously disquieting to the United States, given Cuba's proximity. When <a href="/wiki/Lockheed_U-2" title="Lockheed U-2">Lockheed U-2</a> spy plane flights over the island revealed that Soviet missile launchers were being installed, <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">U.S. President</a> <a href="/wiki/John_F._Kennedy" title="John F. Kennedy">John F. Kennedy</a> instituted a naval <a href="/wiki/Blockade" title="Blockade">blockade</a> and publicly confronted the Soviet Union. After a tense week, the Soviet Union backed down and ordered the launchers removed, not wanting to risk igniting a new world war.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>210<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>211<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="The_space_race">The space race</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Key_events_of_the_20th_century&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: The space race"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Buzz_salutes_the_U.S._Flag.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Buzz_salutes_the_U.S._Flag.jpg/220px-Buzz_salutes_the_U.S._Flag.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="222" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Buzz_salutes_the_U.S._Flag.jpg/330px-Buzz_salutes_the_U.S._Flag.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Buzz_salutes_the_U.S._Flag.jpg/440px-Buzz_salutes_the_U.S._Flag.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3929" data-file-height="3967"></a><figcaption>In 1969, humans first set foot on <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">the Moon</a>.</figcaption></figure>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Space_Race" title="Space Race">Space Race</a></div>
<p>With <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> tensions running high, the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> and United States took their rivalry to the stars in 1957 with the Soviet launch of <a href="/wiki/Sputnik" class="mw-redirect" title="Sputnik">Sputnik</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>212<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> A "space race" between the two powers followed.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>213<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The USSR reached several important milestones, such as the first craft on the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> (<a href="/wiki/Luna_2" title="Luna 2">Luna 2</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>214<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> and the first human in space (<a href="/wiki/Yuri_Gagarin" title="Yuri Gagarin">Yuri Gagarin</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>215<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The U.S. pulled ahead eventually with its <a href="/wiki/Mercury_program" class="mw-redirect" title="Mercury program">Mercury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gemini_program" class="mw-redirect" title="Gemini program">Gemini</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Apollo_program" title="Apollo program">Apollo</a> programs, which culminated in <a href="/wiki/Apollo_11" title="Apollo 11">Apollo 11</a> astronauts landing on the Moon on 20 July 1969.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>216<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Five more landings with astronauts followed (<a href="/wiki/Apollo_13" title="Apollo 13">Apollo 13</a> was forced to abort its mission).<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>217<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Nevertheless, despite its successes, the U.S. space program could not match many major achievements of the Soviet space program, such as <a href="/wiki/Lunokhod_program" class="mw-redirect" title="Lunokhod program">rover-based space exploration</a> and <a href="/wiki/Venera_9" title="Venera 9">image and video transfer</a> from the surface of another planet, until the early 21st century.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>218<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>219<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In addition, both countries launched numerous <a href="/wiki/Space_probe" class="mw-redirect" title="Space probe">probes</a> into space, such as the <a href="/wiki/Venera_7" title="Venera 7">Venera 7</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">Voyager 2</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>220<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In later decades, space became a somewhat friendlier place. Regular space flights with astronauts were made possible with the American <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a>, which was the first reusable spacecraft to be successfully used.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>221<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mir" title="Mir">Mir</a> and <a href="/wiki/Skylab" title="Skylab">Skylab</a> enabled prolonged human habitation in space.<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>222<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>223<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> In the 1990s, work on the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a> began, and by the end of the century, while still incomplete, it was in continual use by astronauts from the United States, Europe, Russia, Japan, and Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>224<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="The_end_of_the_Cold_War">The end of the Cold War</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Key_events_of_the_20th_century&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: The end of the Cold War"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:West_and_East_Germans_at_the_Brandenburg_Gate_in_1989.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/West_and_East_Germans_at_the_Brandenburg_Gate_in_1989.jpg/250px-West_and_East_Germans_at_the_Brandenburg_Gate_in_1989.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/West_and_East_Germans_at_the_Brandenburg_Gate_in_1989.jpg/375px-West_and_East_Germans_at_the_Brandenburg_Gate_in_1989.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/West_and_East_Germans_at_the_Brandenburg_Gate_in_1989.jpg/500px-West_and_East_Germans_at_the_Brandenburg_Gate_in_1989.jpg 2x" data-file-width="661" data-file-height="498"></a><figcaption>In 1989, the <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Wall" title="Berlin Wall">Berlin Wall</a> separating West and East Berlin fell.</figcaption></figure>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cold_War_(1985-1991)" class="mw-redirect" title="Cold War (1985-1991)">Cold War (1985-1991)</a></div>
<p>By the 1980s, the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> was weakening. The <a href="/wiki/Sino-Soviet_split" title="Sino-Soviet split">Sino-Soviet split</a> had removed the USSR's most powerful ally, the People's Republic of China.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>225<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Its <a href="/wiki/Arms_race" title="Arms race">arms race</a> with the U.S. was draining the country of funds, and further weakened by internal pressures, ethnic and political. <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Gorbachev" title="Mikhail Gorbachev">Mikhail Gorbachev</a>, its last leader, attempted to reform the country with <i><a href="/wiki/Glasnost" title="Glasnost">glasnost</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Perestroika" title="Perestroika">perestroika</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>226<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> but the formation of <a href="/wiki/Solidarity_(Polish_trade_union)" title="Solidarity (Polish trade union)">Solidarity</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>227<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> the fall of the <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Wall" title="Berlin Wall">Berlin Wall</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>228<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> and the breaking-off of several Soviet republics, such as <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>229<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> started a slippery slope of events that culminated in a coup to overthrow Gorbachev, organized by Communist Party hard-liners. <a href="/wiki/Boris_Yeltsin" title="Boris Yeltsin">Boris Yeltsin</a>, president of Russia, organized mass opposition, and the coup failed.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>230<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> On 26 December 1991, the Soviet Union was officially disbanded into its constituent republics, thus putting a final line under the already exhausted <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>231<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Information_and_communications_technology">Information and communications technology</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Key_events_of_the_20th_century&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Information and communications technology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Computer" title="Computer">Computer</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_computing_hardware" title="History of computing hardware">History of computing hardware</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Internet" title="Internet">Internet</a></div>
<p>The creation of the <a href="/wiki/Transistor" title="Transistor">transistor</a> revolutionized the development of the computer. The first computers, room-sized electro-mechanical devices built to break <a href="/wiki/Cryptographic" class="mw-redirect" title="Cryptographic">cryptographical</a> codes during World War II,<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>232<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> quickly became at least 20 times smaller using transistors.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>233<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> Computers became <a href="/wiki/Computer_programming" title="Computer programming">reprogrammable</a> rather than fixed-purpose devices. The invention of <a href="/wiki/Programming_language" title="Programming language">programming languages</a> meant computer operators could concentrate on problem solving at a high-level, without having to think in terms of the individual instructions to the computer itself.<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>234<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The creation of <a href="/wiki/Operating_systems" class="mw-redirect" title="Operating systems">operating systems</a> also vastly improved <a href="/wiki/Programming_productivity" title="Programming productivity">programming productivity</a>. Building on this, computer pioneers could now realize what they had envisioned. The <a href="/wiki/Graphical_user_interface" title="Graphical user interface">graphical user interface</a>, piloted by a <a href="/wiki/Computer_mouse" title="Computer mouse">computer mouse</a> made it simple to harness the power of the computer and made it more accessible to new users.<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>235<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Computer_storage" class="mw-redirect" title="Computer storage">Storage</a> for computer programs progressed from <a href="/wiki/Punched_card" title="Punched card">punched cards</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paper_tape" class="mw-redirect" title="Paper tape">paper tape</a> to <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_tape" title="Magnetic tape">magnetic tape</a>, <a href="/wiki/Floppy_disk" title="Floppy disk">floppy disks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hard_disk" class="mw-redirect" title="Hard disk">hard disks</a>. <a href="/wiki/Core_memory" class="mw-redirect" title="Core memory">Core memory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bubble_memory" title="Bubble memory">bubble memory</a> fell to <a href="/wiki/Random_access_memory" class="mw-redirect" title="Random access memory">random access memory</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>236<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Colossus.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Colossus.jpg/220px-Colossus.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Colossus.jpg/330px-Colossus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Colossus.jpg/440px-Colossus.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3504" data-file-height="2337"></a><figcaption>Colossus Mark 2 computer operated by <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_Royal_Naval_Service" title="Women's Royal Naval Service">Wrens</a></figcaption></figure>
<p>The invention of the <a href="/wiki/Word_processor" title="Word processor">word processor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spreadsheet" title="Spreadsheet">spreadsheet</a> and <a href="/wiki/Database" title="Database">database</a> greatly improved office productivity over the old paper, <a href="/wiki/Typewriter" title="Typewriter">typewriter</a> and filing cabinet methods.<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>237<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>238<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The economic advantage given to businesses led to economic efficiencies in computers themselves. Cost-effective <a href="/wiki/Central_processing_unit" title="Central processing unit">CPUs</a> led to thousands of industrial and home-brew computer designs, many of which became successful; a home-computer boom was led by the <a href="/wiki/Apple_II" title="Apple II">Apple II</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Sinclair_ZX80" class="mw-redirect" title="Sinclair ZX80">ZX80</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Commodore_PET" title="Commodore PET">Commodore PET</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>239<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>240<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup>
<a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a>, seeking to embrace the <a href="/wiki/Microcomputer_revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Microcomputer revolution">microcomputer revolution</a>, devised its <a href="/wiki/IBM_Personal_Computer" title="IBM Personal Computer">IBM Personal Computer</a> (PC). Crucially, IBM developed the PC from third-party components that were available on the open market.<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241"><span class="cite-bracket">[</span>241<span class="cite-bracket">]</span></a></sup> The only impediment to another company duplicating the system's architecture was the proprietary <a href="/wiki/BIOS" title="BIOS">BIOS</a> software.
